### Post by chrisbliss18 on 2012-08-03
Hello La Vida thread. I just found out about the game and the funding request via the [lxer link]("http://lxer.com/module/newswire/view/171409/index.html"). It sound like an ambitious project with huge potential.

I have to say though that I didn't feel like I knew enough about what the goals of the project were to plunk down my cash as a backer. Thus the reason that I came here. I fully agree with Sofox that the campaign page could use some improvements. To me, there are four main elements that help me connect with a project (I've backed and am backing numerous projects on Kickstarter):

[list=1]
[*]An informative and/or fun video that features the product, includes narration about what we're seeing, and shows the person/people behind the project.
[*]A nice mix of contribution levels with perks that encourage me to keep thinking about bumping up my contribution.
[*]Timelines that give me confidence that I will know if the project is moving forward or not. As it stands now, you seem to just be saying "give me a year" without saying how we will know that things are progressing or how well they will be progressing.
[*]Word of mouth from other developers.
[/list]

The video that helps me understand where the project is currently, what the end goal of the project is, and who it is that I'm backing. In other words, I want to see any of the current artwork, concepts, and/or test play footage while someone involved in the project talks about it. I also want to see the people behind the project as it helps me connect with actual people rather than just names. I know that this can be daunting for some, but even those that aren't the most comfortable in front of a camera have created some very fun and informative videos.

[Castle Story]("http://www.kickstarter.com/projects/902505202/castle-story") is a current Kickstarter campaign that has a great video. The playtesting video, game music, and interaction with the people behind the project really make it clear what the game is, what it will be, and how dedicated the team is. While a video for La Vida doesn't need to be this caliber, it should give you an idea of what types of things go into a video for a successful project.


Perks that motivate contributions are essential. Setting up your perks properly can make or break your crowd funding campaign. Most of the successful projects that I've seen have a contribution tier starting at $5-10, have at least 5 different contribution tiers that build on one another (the second tier gives you everything in the first tier, the third tier gives you everything in the first and second tiers, etc), have tiers that start off with relatively small differences in price and that get larger as it goes up (10, 20, 30, 50, 75, etc), and have elements in the perks that really push people to want to bump up their contributions.

The [Zombie Playground]("http://www.kickstarter.com/projects/massiveblack/zombie-playground-zpg-3d-action-online-battle-rpg?ref=users") Kickstarter campaign shows how you can set up perks at nearly every $5 increment between $5 and $100. Even though its $5 and $10 levels didn't really include anything that costs the developers money, they still got $420 for those two tiers. That's $420 by just asking for it.

The two primary problems you have with your current perks are 1) they do not encourage an impulse contribution and 2) the minimum perk contribution is too high.

Anyone can make a contribution of any value. However, most people don't know this. They will look at the perks, find one that works for them, and select it. If there isn't one that offers what they want or that sounds interesting at a reasonable price, they will move on.

First, you need a perk starting at no more than $5-10. It's not clear if the game will be free as in beer or just free as in speech. If the game itself is not free, then make this first tier how a contributor purchases the game. If the game itself is free, then make this first tier something simple like getting listed in the game's credits as a contributor.

Second, you need to have a minimum of 5 perk levels spanning through the $100 level. For instance: $10, $20, $30, $50, $75, and $100. The types of perks you can offer will determine the prices and number of perks to have in this range.

The point of the perks is to get the person thinking "only $10 more and I can get that awesome thing," "ohh.... only $20 more and I get that as well," "ohhh OHHH... only $30 more for all of that plus this awesome thing." If you get them thinking like this, then you've hooked them and will get some money. If you don't, you've lost a potential investor or the potential to get them to contribute more.


While this thread may be the home of La Vida development, most people are not going to read through 4 years of posts to find out about the game. They aren't likely to do it now, and they aren't likely to do so going forward. So, you need to create a proper home for this project. In other words, it needs its own site.

The new La Vida site should have a blog that you and other members of the project post to on at least a weekly basis talking about new developments complete with videos and screenshots. It should also feature a community blog where people can provide feedback, ask questions, and start providing content and resources. Since the project is focused on open source ideals, why not host the project on [github]("https://github.com/") in order to allow people to easily bug test, play test, and provide patches. This github project can then be linked to from the site complete with documentation that shows how people can contribute.

Remember that to be successful, you need to have more than just developers contributing to the project. You need gamers that want to play the game yet don't know the difference between a polygon mesh and a nested conditional. The only way to attract this crowd is to have resources that aren't developer focused.


You need to start networking with other indie game developers. Having another developer link to your campaign can quickly feed it with a new batch of ready and willing contributors.

I've seen this many times on Kickstarter. A project doesn't look like it will make its goal and there are only a couple of days left in the campaign. Some posts and tweets from other project developers quickly expose that project to new contributors, and it easily meets its goal.


This sounds like a lot of work, and it is, but if you are wanting to get funding for your next year of living expenses, then I suggest that you take this project out of the hobby project realm and start to make it look like a serious and professional project, complete with everything that people expect to see from serious projects.

Just my two cents.

---

### Post by MElawe on 2012-08-06
Is this project being developed yet?


> **RIchard James13 said:**
> As a programmer I have this to say. Read this [article]("http://www.moddb.com/tutorials/24591/the-modular-release-model") for making your goals [SMART]("http://www.topachievement.com/smart.html"). 

Most games developed in the commercial world are based on a thing called  a design document. This is normally a book, a few hundred pages of how  everything works in the game. Of course no game starts out at a few  hundred pages. You start with some simple ideas and then you flesh them  out. If you are unable to program you might be able to do art. But the  most important thing FOSS projects need is someone willing to lead and  to persist until the job is done. In order to lead people you need a  direction and that is where the design document comes in. The more  detailed the document the more easier it is for programmers and artists  to complete the work. 

I must warn you that having a design document will not bring programmers  and artist to your project. You will need other ways to entice them in.  People need a way to contribute (sourceforge or launchpad) and a way to  communicate (mailing lists, IRC channels, Forums).

Now for an example of how to start fleshing the game out.
[LIST]
[*]Single or MultiPlayer?
[*]Isometric or 3D?
[*]What are you going to call your sim people? (can't be sims)
[*]What are some items of clothing they might wear?
[*]Do they have houses? How many rooms? Are they going to be multi-level?
[*]Are there doors? How do they work?
[*]What will the walls and windows look like?
[*]Can the simulated people have jobs? How can they spend their income?
[*]What  is the user interface like? How does the player control their  character? Can they control more than one character at a time?
[*]Will the game have a loading screen?
[*]Will you have ambient music?
[*]Will you have sound effects?
[*]What simulated emotions will the people have? How will the player be able to tell these emotions?
[/LIST]
Remember  don't answer like in the Sims. You need to write and/or draw how  everything will work. As in the article above don't add too much stuff  to your game at once. Start with a few small goals but be very specific  as how they could be achieved. Most of the successful FOSS games started  with very little but with dedication over the years have built up a  significant game.

Thanks for the links! :)

-MElawe
*JClass* Dev Team
*JClass, Owner, Founder*

### Post by madjr on 2012-08-07

### Post by Sofox on 2012-09-06
Hey Mike,

Sorry your campaign wasn't more successful, but congratulations on the money you did raise and I admire how you perservered and continually improved the campaign as it went on; and how you plan to go for it again after Kickstarter opens up.

A guy suggested some guides about creating Kickstarter campaigns that I thought might help you:

[http://www.altdevblogaday.com/2012/09/04/want-to-get-kickstarted-heres-what-you-need-to-know/](http://www.altdevblogaday.com/2012/09/04/want-to-get-kickstarted-heres-what-you-need-to-know/)
 
[http://www.thekickbackmachine.com/](http://www.thekickbackmachine.com/)

Kickstarter is going to be tough, if you don't hit the target you don't get anything. I'd really suggest you create the campaign text, reward tiers and video and then get feedback on them before you start the campaign. Your campaign page should be as good as it can be before the campaign starts, and I and I'm sure many other here would be happy to give you feedback and advice.

Regards, and best of luck with your work and future campaign,
Sofox

### Post by Mickeysofine1972 on 2012-09-08
Ok Just so everyone knows here is the run down from indiegogo.com


Summary: Amount Raised: $270 Amount Disbursed: $151

Disbursement Calculation:

Amount Raised by Credit Card: $200

Indiegogo Platform fee (9.0%): $18

Payment processing fee (3.0%): $6

International wire transfer fee: $25

+ Goal Completion Refund (5.0%): $0 = Net Credit Card Funds: $151

Amount Raised by PayPal: $70

Indiegogo Platform fee (9.0%): $6

Estimated payment processing fee (3.0%): $2

+ Goal Completion Refund (5.0%): $0 = Net PayPal Funds: $61

Total Disbursement Net Credit Card Funds: $151 + PayPal Goal Completion Refund: $0 = Total Amount Disbursed to Account: $151

Its safe to say that I would not recommend anyone use them! 

Mike

---

### Post by Sofox on 2012-10-09
He raised $270, and received $151 dollars.

This means he actually received 55% of the total raised.

9.26% of this was a fixed cost ($25 wire transfer fee)

He could have gained another $61 (22.6%) from the Paypal funds, but for some reason he doesn't seem to have got them. From what he typed, the reason for not getting it seems to be that he didn't reach the goal.

If he hadn't the wire transfer fee or and had received the Paypal funds, he would have gotten $237, which would have been 87.78% of total raised funds.

So essentially, one of the major reasons he didn't get a high percentage was because the total amount was so low it got eaten up by a fixed cost, and the other reason seems to be that he didn't reach his target.

The rest of the fees seem to be standard charages that always crop up on stuff like this. Kickstarter also has it's own charges and a Paypal fee.

### Post by Mickeysofine1972 on 2013-02-23
Hi All!

Good news! The project is going to take a few turns and twists so that we can get things back on a roll.

The first thing I want to mention is that for support reasons I'm switching the development language from Python, (which is awesome), to Unity3d so that we can support as many platforms as possible including Linux.

The second is that I've spent the contributions from the last campaign in getting some concept art and logo design done as well as involving a 3d artist so that we can start and generate some awesome content.

We're preparing to run a campaign on kickstarter.com but that is a few months away so for the time being if you would like to keep up to date with regular posts and demo videos please go to our facebook page [http://www.facebook.com/pages/La-Vida/165050630296998](http://www.facebook.com/pages/La-Vida/165050630296998) where you'll be able to see all our progress as we move towards getting funding.

We will be posting at least twice a week if not more to show things as they happen so please come over!

Mike

### Post by Sofox on 2013-02-24
Hey Mickey, best of luck with the campaign.

I am a bit disappointed you're dropping the open source aspect, but since you're the main guy who's pushing this forward and doing all the work, it's completely your call and I wish you the best!

---

### Post by Mickeysofine1972 on 2013-02-24
> **Sofox said:**
> Hey Mickey, best of luck with the campaign.

I am a bit disappointed you're dropping the open source aspect, but since you're the main guy who's pushing this forward and doing all the work, it's completely your call and I wish you the best!

Ah! but will I?

To be honest I would like to earn a living whilst developing the game but there's nothing to say I wont opensource later. A bit like Id did with doom and quake.

Plus, for the time being there are mostly people who want to play the game on linux and not so many looking to program, hence the choice of Unity3d as I can develop by myself on that platform while there arent many people looking to help develop the game.

So releasing the source doesn't seem to be the main concern for the project right now, and if enough people want it I may well do so. 

I suppose it all depends on the level of support the game gets from the linux community when its time to for the kickstarter. If the community shows support then it will have a game to play first then later some code to extend and learn from :)

Lets see what happens :)

Mike
